wp-image-617566 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-matrix-5-e1589461630807-700x337.jpg\" alt=\"the matrix 4 production\" width=\"700\" height=\"337\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-matrix-5-e1589461630807-700x337.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-matrix-5-e1589461630807-360x174.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-matrix-5-e1589461630807-768x370.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-matrix-5-e1589461630807.jpg 780w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><em>(Welcome to <a href=\"https://slashfilm.com/tag/and-more\"><strong>…And More</strong></a>, our no-frills, zero B.S. guide to when and where you can watch upcoming movies and shows, and everything else you could possibly stand to know.)</em></p>\n<p><strong>Lilly</strong> and <strong>Lana Wachowski</strong>&#8216;s 1999 science-fiction action blockbuster <em><strong>The Matrix</strong></em> is possibly the most famous cyberpunk property in existence. Now, 22 years and two sequels later, we&#8217;re ready to take the red pill once more and return to cyberspace in <em><strong>The Matrix 4</strong></em>. Here&#8217;s everything we know about <em>The Matrix 4</em>.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-681128\"></span></p>\n<h2><strong>The Matrix 4 Release Date and Where You Can Watch It</strong></h2>\n<p><em>The Matrix 4 </em>has gone through its share of COVID-related delays, much like any other film from the past year and a half. The original release date was May 21, 2021, though that <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-matrix-4-release-date-delay/\">got pushed because of the pandemic</a>.</p>\n<p>While <em>The Matrix 4</em> is probably the kind of action thrill-ride that merits a trip to the theater, fans will be able to enjoy it at home on the same day as well. As part of the HBOMax same day release schedule, <em>The Matrix 4</em> slides into theaters and HBOMax on <strong>December 22, 2021</strong>. Fans who want to bullet-dodge with Neo and co. from the comfort of their couch will have to act fast, however, as <em>The Matrix 4</em> will only stream on the service for 31 days.</p>\n<h2>What is The Matrix 4?</h2>\n<p><em>The Matrix 4</em> is the fourth installment in the <em>Matrix</em> series, which started with <em>The Matrix </em>in 1999 and was summarily followed by <em><strong>The Matrix: Reloaded</strong></em><strong> </strong>and <em><strong>The Matrix: Revolutions </strong></em>in 2003. The first film told the story of Neo (<strong>Keanu Reeves</strong>), a hacker who turns out to be the prophesied hero of mankind. Following a war between humanity and the machines they built, the machines put everyone in tubes and connected them to the matrix, a kind of virtual reality that people believe is reality. Neo must fight the computer program Agent Smith<em><strong> </strong></em>(<strong>Hugo Weaving</strong>) with the help of his mentor, Morpheus (<strong>Laurence Fishburne</strong>) and his new romantic interest, Trinity (<strong>Carrie-Anne Moss</strong>). After defeating the machines and gaining some superhuman powers at the end of the first film, Neo must go up against bigger adversaries in the second and third installments.</p>\n<p>The sequels were not particularly well-received by critics or audiences, though there has been a reappraisal of them in recent years. <em>The Matrix: Revolutions</em> ended with Neo sacrificing himself to connect the machines to himself, hopefully to create lasting peace. Details on the plot of <em>The Matrix 4</em> have been kept extremely secret, leaving us to guess at how they might bring back the machine-ending messiah.</p>\n<h2>The Matrix 4 Director, Crew, and More</h2>\n<p>Writer and director <strong>Lana Wachowski</strong> will return to direct <em>The Matrix 4</em>. She co-wrote the script with novelists <strong>Aleksandar Hemon</strong> and <strong>David Mitchell</strong>, whom she previously worked with on the Netflix series <strong><em>Sense8</em></strong>. (She also made a movie of Mitchell&#8217;s <strong><em>Cloud Atlas</em></strong>, a novel once deemed unfilmable.) <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-matrix-4-cinematographer-john-toll/\">Oscar-winning cinematographer </a><strong>John Toll </strong>(<em><strong>Braveheart</strong></em>) will take over for  Bill Pope as director of photography. Concept artists for the <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-matrix-concept-artists-the-matrix-4/\">original films <strong>Geof Darrow</strong> and <strong>Steve Skroce</strong> will return</a>, which means <em>The Matrix 4</em> will have their signature cybernetic visual designs.</p>\n<h2>The Matrix 4 Cast</h2>\n<p>Quite a few members of the cast from the original trilogy are returning. Reeves and Moss are returning as Neo and Trinity, and <strong>Jada Pinkett-Smith</strong> is returning as hovercraft captain Niobe. <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-matrix-4-morpheus-laurence-fishburne-filming/\">Fishburne will not be reprising his role as Morpheus</a>, however, and <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-matrix-4-hugo-weaving/\">Weaving will not return as Agent Smith</a>.</p>\n<p>There will be plenty of new faces in <em>The Matrix 4</em>, however, including <strong>Christina Ricci</strong>, <strong>Neil Patrick Harris</strong>, <strong>Priyanka Chopra</strong>, <strong>Jessica Henwick</strong>, <strong>Toby Onwumere</strong>, <strong>Brian J. Smith</strong>, <strong>Eréndira Ibarra</strong>, <strong>Andrew Caldwell</strong>, <strong>Yahya Abdul-Mateen II</strong>, and <strong>Jonathan Groff</strong>. We have no idea who any of them are portraying, though some outlets have reported that  Abdul-Mateen II will play a younger version of Morpheus in flashbacks.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-matrix-4-release-date-cast-and-more/\">&#8216;The Matrix 4&#8217;: Release Date, Cast and More</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?a=Kd7UridHQ8E:quTLI8acdVU:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img 02:35:16","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:16","title":"‘Thor: Love and Thunder’ is So Crazy “It’s Almost Like It Shouldn’t Be Made,” Teases Director Taika Waititi","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=682429","description":"<p><img class=\"aligncenter wp-image-624062 size-medium\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/taika-waititi-e1593184146819-700x355.jpg\" alt=\"thor love and thunder tease\" width=\"700\" height=\"355\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/taika-waititi-e1593184146819-700x355.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/taika-waititi-e1593184146819-360x183.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/taika-waititi-e1593184146819-768x390.jpg 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>Some filmmakers can be prone to hyperbole at times, providing quotes that wildly exaggerate their intentions with any given movie to make for some juicy headlines and press cycles. We would humbly propose that <strong><em>Thor: Love and Thunder</em> </strong>director <strong>Taika Waititi</strong>, on the other hand, tends to say exactly what he means and mean exactly what he says. In his own endearing way, at least.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-682429\"></span></p>\n<h2>The World According To Taika</h2>\n<p>In an interview with <a href=\"https://www.empireonline.com/movies/news/thor-love-thunder-shouldnt-make-sense-taika-waititi-exclusive/\">Empire</a> (<a href=\"https://collider.com/taika-waititi-thor-love-and-thunder-comments/\">via Collider</a>), the popular and eccentric director of <em><strong>Boy</strong></em>, <strong><em>Hunt for the Wilderpeople</em></strong>, and <em><strong>Thor: Ragnarok</strong></em> has provided a fresh tease for what he has in store for us with <em><strong>Thor: Love and Thunder</strong></em>. Based on his quotes, he has lost none of his usual penchant for bravado and childlike excitement:</p>\n<blockquote><p>&#8220;Well, just between (me and) you and the readers, I’ve done some crazy shit in my life. I’ve lived, like, 10 lifetimes. But it’s the craziest film I’ve ever done. If you wrote down all the elements of this film, it shouldn’t make sense. It’s almost like it shouldn’t be made. If you walked into a room and said, ‘I want this and this and this.’ Who’s in it? These people. What are you going to call it? Love And Thunder. I mean, you’d never work again. Maybe I won’t after this.&#8221;</p></blockquote>\n<p>Honestly, this sounds like a filmmaker who simply can&#8217;t believe he&#8217;s found himself in a position to put his distinctive touch on a big-budget blockbuster on the scale of a Marvel movie&#8230; and not just once, but <em>twice</em>. Given his indie New Zealand roots that started with an exploration into his childhood love for Michael Jackson songs and then moved on to a farcical &#8220;documentary&#8221; of the local vampire/werewolf scene (seriously, we implore you to watch <em>Boy</em> and <em><strong>What We Do In The Shadows</strong></em> if you haven&#8217;t yet), Waititi goes on to assure viewers that this <em>Thor</em> sequel will stand apart from <em>Ragnarok:</em></p>\n<blockquote><p>&#8220;It’s very different from Ragnarok. It’s crazier. I’ll tell you what’s different. There’ll be far more emotion in this film. And a lot more love. And a lot more thunder. And a lot more Thor, if you’ve seen the photos.&#8221;</p></blockquote>\n<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-464204 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/thorragnarok-taikawaititi-thor-onset-700x326.jpg\" alt=\"Thor Ragnarok Intro Video - Taika Waititi\" width=\"700\" height=\"326\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/thorragnarok-taikawaititi-thor-onset-700x326.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/thorragnarok-taikawaititi-thor-onset-360x168.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/thorragnarok-taikawaititi-thor-onset-768x358.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/thorragnarok-taikawaititi-thor-onset.jpg 1380w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<h2>What to Expect in Thor: Love and Thunder</h2>\n<p>After using <em>Ragnarok</em> to unleash <strong>Tessa Thompson&#8217;s</strong> fan-favorite Valkyrie, goth <strong>Cate Blanchett</strong>, and a face-painted <strong>Jeff Goldblum</strong> who might as well have wandered onto the set entirely by accident one day and remained in the film anyway, needless to say we can&#8217;t wait to see how Waititi outdoes himself for the upcoming 2022 movie. It&#8217;s hard not to feel energized by his own infectious excitement and, honestly, it&#8217;s just nice to see a filmmaker remain enthused to provide entertainment to the masses.</p>\n<p><em>Thor: Love and Thunder</em> will feature returning stars <strong>Chris Hemsworth</strong>, Tessa Thompson, Jeff Goldblum, and even a Mjolnir-wielding <strong>Natalie Portman</strong> as Jane Foster. They&#8217;ll be joined by <strong>Chris Pratt</strong> and many of the Guardians of the Galaxy, not to mention a villainous turn by <strong>Christian Bale</strong> (of all people!) as Gorr The God Butcher. Yeah, that&#8217;ll do.</p>\n<p>The highly-anticipated <em>Thor</em> sequel is set for a <strong>May 6, 2022</strong> release.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/thor-love-and-thunder-tease-taika-waititi/\">&#8216;Thor: Love and Thunder&#8217; is So Crazy &#8220;It&#8217;s Almost Like It Shouldn&#8217;t Be Made,&#8221; Teases Director Taika Waititi</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a 02:35:16","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:16","title":"‘Mobile Suit Gundam’ More Than Lives Up to Its Influential Reputation as One of the Anime Greats","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=682405","description":"<p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-682407\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Mobile-Suit-Gundam-700x300.png\" alt=\"Mobile Suit Gundam\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Mobile-Suit-Gundam-700x300.png 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Mobile-Suit-Gundam-360x154.png 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Mobile-Suit-Gundam-768x329.png 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Mobile-Suit-Gundam.png 1200w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">(Welcome to </span></i><b><i>Ani-time Ani-where</i></b><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, a regular column dedicated to helping the uninitiated understand and appreciate the world of anime.)</span></i></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The </span><a href=\"https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Main/SeinfeldIsUnfunny\"><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Seinfeld</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> is Unfunny</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> effect is a trope that refers to the feeling of watching a work that is so influential that you recognize everything it once pioneered, but are no longer impressed by it. The jokes aren&#8217;t funny anymore, and you see the twists coming a mile away. Some works, however, manage to live up to their reputation and impress even decades later. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The original </span><b><i>Mobile Suit Gundam </i></b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">is one such work.</span></p>\n<p><span id=\"more-682405\"></span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">We&#8217;ve covered this massively popular franchise </span><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/mobile-suit-gundam-the-08th-ms-team/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">once before</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, but now the show that started it all is finally available to stream. So before Netflix makes a </span><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/gundam-live-action-movie-netflix-jordan-vogt-roberts/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">live-action movie</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, we’re heading back to the beginning to explore the show that kickstarted what would become Japan&#8217;s answer to </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Star Wars</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">; a franchise as iconic as it is inaccessible given how many spin-offs, sequels, prequels, and myriad alternate universes it generated. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The show takes us to the tail end of </span><a href=\"https://gundam.fandom.com/wiki/One_Year_War\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">the One Year War</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, a conflict between a unified </span><a href=\"https://gundam.fandom.com/wiki/Earth_Federation\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Earth Federation</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> and a group of rebels from space colonies known as </span><a href=\"https://gundam.fandom.com/wiki/Principality_of_Zeon\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">the Principality of Zeon</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">. The story focuses on a teenage boy named Amuro Ray whose father helps create a new mobile suit called (you guessed it) Gundam to fight Zeon&#8217;s mobile suits. Amuro inadvertently becomes the sole pilot of the titular suit and gets sucked into the war with a group of child soldiers aboard a giant spaceship. Char Aznable, a brooding flying ace also known as the Red Comet, becomes Amuro&#8217;s rival while keeping a secret mission to reclaim his honor. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Whether you want to experience the entire 43-episode show or watch the condensed seven-hour movie trilogy, let&#8217;s get in the robot and explore the show that changed the mecha genre forever. </span></p>\n<h2><b>What Makes It Great</b></h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">From the start, you get the feeling that you’re watching a small chapter in a much larger epic saga. Contrary to the grand operatic battles, the command center intrigue, and the throne room machinations akin to </span><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/legend-of-the-galactic-heroes/\"><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Legend of the Galactic Heroes</span></i></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, creator Yoshiyuki Tomino decides to focus the action primarily on the contained story of the spaceship White Base and its crew. We occasionally glimpse what command has in store and only get rare wide shots of the space battle, giving us a child soldier&#8217;s perspective of events they have no control over.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">And when I say child, I mean it. The crew of White Base is composed entirely of teenagers, young adults, and a few kids, as they were the only survivors of an attack in their home colony. </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Gundam </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">was very clearly created by people who grew up in a post-WWII Japan, having seen countless adults go out to fight and never return, leaving the young generation to pick up the pieces. We see how they try their best to distract themselves from the horrors that surround them, and how they are forced to grow up because of it. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Watching </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Gundam </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">today, you can see how the characters influenced countless archetypes in young adult fiction, with Amuro serving as a clear template for </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Neon Genesis Evangelion</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">&#8216;s Shinji Ikari; both are teenagers tasked with manning an incredible weapon and charging into war. Meanwhile, Char&#8217;s backstory and pseudo-face-turn bring to mind </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Avatar: The Last Airbender </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">and Zuko&#8217;s redemption arc.</span></p>\n<h2><b>What It Adds to the Conversation</b></h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Arguably </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Gundam</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">&#8216;s greatest contribution to the mecha genre is its treatment of war and its human cost. No matter how cool the Gundam fights are — if watching Jedi fights in </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Star Wars </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">is cool, try giving a lightsaber to a giant robot — the show always makes sure to cut away to the devastating losses that surround the cool moments. None of the main characters make it to the other side unscathed as the death and violence start taking a heavy toll on our incredibly young ensemble. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">This is why the show&#8217;s focus on a small group of common soldiers rather than commanders and leaders is so effective: it shows us that the ones doing the fighting and the dying are just regular people. That this was done on a kids’ show back in the 70s at a time when anime (like American cartoons during the &#8217;80s) was mostly made to sell toys is still an impressive achievement.</span></p>\n<p><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Gundam</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">&#8216;s</span> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">anti-war message is also delivered through the show&#8217;s weirdest and most sci-fi concept — </span><a href=\"https://gundam.fandom.com/wiki/Newtype\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">the Newtypes</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, humans who have evolved to adapt to life in space with heightened senses. Though this first series doesn&#8217;t do much with the concept, the few explanations we get hints at Tomino&#8217;s ideas that humanity will eventually evolve to be capable of understanding one another, therefore avoiding conflict. It&#8217;s not entirely unlike </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Evangelion</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">&#8216;s Human Instrumentality, and it complements </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Gundam</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">&#8216;s anti-war themes nicely as the Newtypes are mostly used as weapons of war by people in charge with older and more limited ways of thinking.</span></p>\n<h2><b>Why Non-Anime Fans Should Check It Out</b></h2>\n<p><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Mobile Suit Gundam</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> is the quintessential anime franchise and this first series is the original </span><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mecha_anime_and_manga#Real_robot\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">real robot</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> show. An epic yet intimate coming-of-age story in the middle of a galactic war with incredible action, fantastic world-building, and relatable characters. Whether you&#8217;ve never seen a mecha anime before, are looking for a more grounded space opera, or want to see the show that birthed an entire sub-genre, now&#8217;s as good a time as any to enter the Universal Century timeline and watch some </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Gundam</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">.</span></p>\n<p><b>Watch This If You Like:</b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> Star Wars, Neon Genesis Evangelion, Giant robots wielding laser swords, Code Geass.</span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">***</p>\n<p><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Mobile Suit Gundam </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">is streaming on Crunchyroll and Funimation. The </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Gundam </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">compilation trilogy is streaming on Netflix.</span></p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/mobile-suit-gundam/\">&#8216;Mobile Suit Gundam&#8217; More Than Lives Up to Its Influential Reputation as One of the Anime Greats</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?a=yI28sPSJ7Xs:IDzrzqUlPgU:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img +0000","feedId":21,"bgimg":"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Mobile-Suit-Gundam-700x300.png","linkMd5":"5126cec5e149603e900f9018501841ce","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn48@2020_1/2021/07/12/18-35-34-457_505bffa6bd1091d0.webp","destWidth":700,"destHeight":300,"sourceBytes":380540,"destBytes":26006,"author":"Rafael Motamayor","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Mobile-Suit-Gundam-700x300.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn48@2020_1/2021/07/12/18-35-34-457_505bffa6bd1091d0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn75@2020_4/2021/07/12/18-35-39-281_9a442e8760d02cd4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=yI28sPSJ7Xs:IDzrzqUlPgU:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn34@2020_4/2021/07/12/18-35-38-806_bcff9237ad0e7834.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=I9og5sOYxJI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn20@2020_1/2021/07/12/18-35-39-646_879c5505f034c3d2.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=yI28sPSJ7Xs:IDzrzqUlPgU:EGzC5_Ws_ZE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn37@2020_6/2021/07/12/18-35-38-807_7047050126b54a61.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/slashfilm/~4/yI28sPSJ7Xs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn8@2020_6/2021/07/12/18-35-38-776_da411a89574fc7dd.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1626114916156},{"createdTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:16","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:16","title":"‘After Yang’ Review: Kogonada’s Second Film is a Quiet Sci-Fi Stunner to Be Savored [Cannes]","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=682416","description":"<p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-682417\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/after-yang-e1626093424162-700x299.jpeg\" alt=\"after yang review\" width=\"700\" height=\"299\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/after-yang-e1626093424162-700x300.jpeg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/after-yang-e1626093424162-360x154.jpeg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/after-yang-e1626093424162-768x328.jpeg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/after-yang-e1626093424162.jpeg 1004w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><strong><em>After Yang</em></strong> begins inside a tea shop, an austere space with a decidedly Japanese aesthetic. Jake (<strong>Colin Farrell</strong>) greets a patron who has walked down the stairs, seeking out “tea crystals” for instant pleasure. Explaining that the local supermarket may carry such convenient goods, and that instead he’s got preparations that may require more effort but may be more rewarding, she leaves frustrated, wondering how someone can survive by not giving the customer what they already want.</p>\n<p>This interaction in many ways echoes the slow, methodical, yet absolutely stellar cinema of the mononymic <strong>Kogonada</strong>, the Korean-American video essayist and Criterion supplement master whose <em>Columbus</em> was one of the triumphs of Sundance 2017. His latest, <em>After Yang</em>, is a near-future tale of a remarkable family, with Jake joined by his wife of African descent Kyra (Jodie Turner-Smith), raising their adopted daughter Mika (<strong>Malea Emma Tjandrawidjaja</strong>). They buy a second-hand “technosapien,” essentially a life-like android or replicant, that’s named Yang (<strong>Justin H. Min</strong>) to instill in their daughter knowledge of her Chinese heritage.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-682416\"></span></p>\n<p>Told through a series of quiet conversations, either in simply staged rooms or while traveling in driverless vehicles, there’s little in the way of kinetic flourish to either storytelling or the performances. Yet Kogonada&#8217;s precision, akin to the blend we witness the master tea maker assembling, prodding gently with a wooden utensil to craft a perfect mix of ingredients, results in a heady brew that’s both intoxicating and delicious.</p>\n<p>Farrell has often been drawn to offbeat dramas like this, as evidenced by his work with another Cannes favorite Yorgos Lanthimos. Here he brings more of his trademark subtlety of performance, managing in often elegant ways to be paradoxically comforting and surprising at the same time. Turner-Smith is both formidable and nurturing, and Min’s role as the aloof android would easily appear to be rote, but here it’s injected with the humanity of the character dialed in perfectly. Tjandrawidjaja is the precocious heart of the narrative, and here too Kogonada manages to elicit a performance perfectly attuned to the story’s requirements. <strong>Haley Lu Richardson</strong> plays Ada, whose storyline becomes entwined with the family, and the ever welcome <strong>Clifton Collins Jr.</strong> plays George, their gormless but well meaning neighbor.</p>\n<p>Written by short-story author <strong>Alexander Weinstein</strong> and based on his own novel, the script delights every moment that genre expectations are undercut. Each moment where you’d expect some emotional explosion, dire conspiracy sidebar, or even expected violence, you’re instead treated to something that feels very real and very earned, making the near-future setting’s fantastic technologies feel all the more quotidian. This is a film whose absences drive much of its pleasure, from the memories of characters and times now gone, to the lack of these story beats that a lesser script (or a more twitchy studio) would add to spice things up.</p>\n<p>Instead, we have a film whose pace may be quieted by, nonetheless is driven forward in both an entertaining and intellectually stimulating fashion. This is no small magic trick, and just as with <em>Columbus,</em> it’s clear that Kogonada is gifted at doing contemplative without compromising audience engagement. At a festival full of meanderings in place of genuine plot, all to occupy time rather than drive a story forward in a cinematically compelling way, it’s certainly worthy of celebration with a master filmmaker can lead us along in a gentle but guided fashion.</p>\n<p>Like the tea, this is a film to be sipped and savored, to appreciate the ritual of watching, and to be wowed by the quiet yet eloquent execution. It’s a film that borrows liberally from the genre, yet does so not in a winking fashion, but more like putting on a comfortable garment, something to drape over oneself in a way both warming and familiar. Yet <em>After Yang</em> still feels extremely fresh and present, further cementing Kogonada as a true talent to cherish, and presenting a storyline that may be slightly bitter to some craving the more conventional crystals, but for those with a taste for such a blend they will find the experience nothing short of thrilling.</p>\n<p><strong>/Film Rating: 9 out of 10</strong></p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/after-yang-review-cannes/\">&#8216;After Yang&#8217; Review: Kogonada&#8217;s Second Film is a Quiet Sci-Fi Stunner to Be Savored [Cannes]</a> 02:35:16","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:16","title":"‘Looney Tunes Cartoon’ Season 2 Gives Elmer Fudd His Gun Back","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=682813","description":"<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-682853 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/looneytunescartoons-season2-elmerfudd-gun-700x300.jpg\" alt=\"Looney Tunes Cartoons Guns Back\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/looneytunescartoons-season2-elmerfudd-gun.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/looneytunescartoons-season2-elmerfudd-gun-360x154.jpg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>When HBO Max debuted their new series of <strong><em>Looney Tunes Cartoons</em></strong> featuring revamped versions of the classic Warner Bros. Pictures characters, there was one key element missing from two of Bugs Bunny&#8217;s most famous enemies. Elmer Fudd and Yosemite Sam <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/hbo-max-looney-tunes-cartoons-avoid-guns/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">weren&#8217;t seen using guns</a> despite always blasting away at the wascally wabbit/lily-livered, bow-legged varmint in the classic cartoons. However, <em>Looney Tunes Cartoons</em> season 2 has pulled back on that a bit by giving Elmer Fudd his trusty rifle back.<span id=\"more-682813\"></span></p>\n<p>The lack of guns in the first season of <em>Looney Tunes Cartoons</em> was confirmed by executive producer <strong>Peter Browngardt</strong> not long after the first wave of animated shorts was released by HBO Max. Though he didn&#8217;t cite any specific motivation about the exclusion of guns for Elmer Fudd and Yosemite Sam, it was assumed that the social climate surrounding gun violence was likely an influence. As Browngardt said, &#8220;We’re not doing guns. But we can do cartoony violence — TNT, the Acme stuff. All that was kind of grandfathered in.&#8221;</p>\n<p>It appears that Browngardt had a change of heart, because <a href=\"https://www.cbr.com/hbo-max-looney-tunes-cartoons-season-2-elmer-fudd-guns/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Comic Book Resources</a> points out that the sixth episode of the second season, entitled &#8220;Rotund Rabbit,&#8221; features Bugs Bunny squaring off with Elmer Fudd again, but this time he&#8217;s carrying his trusty hunting rifle. In the cartoon, Elmer Fudd tries to blast Bugs Bunny, but the rabbit is so fast that the cartoon bullet stops in the middle of its trajectory, sprouts a mouth and arms, and exasperatedly announces that he&#8217;s quitting the chase. The bullet then tosses on a hat and grabs a suitcase and leaves.</p>\n<p>Browngardt pointed out the change when a user on Twitter noticed the backtracking on keeping guns out of <em>Looney Tunes</em>, including the aforementioned clip in question:</p>\n<blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n<p lang=\"en\" dir=\"ltr\">New Looney Tunes Cartoons out today! Now with guns! <a href=\"https://t.co/YG2dJU1AaT\">https://t.co/YG2dJU1AaT</a></p>\n<p>&#8212; Pete Browngardt (@peebgardt) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/peebgardt/status/1413145069364805640?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">July 8, 2021</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><script async src=\"https://platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script></p>\n<h2>Elmer Get Your Gun</h2>\n<p>Though there was a lot of backlash from certain <em>Looney Tunes</em> fans after it was pointed out that guns wouldn&#8217;t appear in the first season of <em>Looney Tunes Cartoons</em>, the inclusion of Elmer Fudd&#8217;s gun again may not be a reaction intended to appease that side of the crowd. Cartoons take a long time to make, and one of the segments included in the recently released second season, entitled &#8220;Mummy Dummy,&#8221; actually debuted at the Annecy International Animation Film Festival in 2019. So perhaps bringing <em>Looney Tunes Cartoons</em> guns back was always something that would happen eventually.</p>\n<p>Furthermore, it&#8217;s not as if those behind the <em>Looney Tunes</em> had actively abandoned the use of cartoon guns in the hands of Elmer Fudd and Yosemite Sam altogether. <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/tag/space-jam-a-new-legacy/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>Space Jam: A New Legacy</em></strong></a> marketing materials have clearly shown both of the characters wielding guns again, so there&#8217;s not an overarching mandate to keep them out of these shorts. As prominent of a problem gun violence might be, keeping them out of cartoons that have had them for decades isn&#8217;t going to fix the problem. That&#8217;s up to the incompetent fools in our government who are all a bunch of Elmer Fudds and Yosemite Sams.</p>\n<p>The first and second seasons of <em>Looney Tunes Cartoons</em> are available now on HBO Max.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/looney-tunes-cartoons-guns-back/\">&#8216;Looney Tunes Cartoon&#8217; Season 2 Gives Elmer Fudd His Gun Back</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?a=VFwO_ZHHiBc:BK0EKENJSzw:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=yIl2AUoC8zA\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a Taskmaster","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=682846","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-676104 aligncenter\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><strong><em>Black Widow</em></strong> had a <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/black-widow-box-office-pandemic-record/\">blockbuster (by pandemic standards) opening weekend</a>, with millions of Marvel fans around the world catching the latest Marvel Cinematic Universe film in theaters or via Premier access on Disney+.</p>\n<p>That also means that millions are also in the know about the backstory of the formidable <strong>Taskmaster</strong>, a warrior who ruthlessly goes after Natasha Romanoff (<strong>Scarlett Johansson</strong>) and can emulate the fighting styles of more than one Avenger.</p>\n<p>/Film talked with <em>Black Widow</em> screenwriter <strong>Eric Pearson</strong> about his inspiration for creating the MCU’s version of Taskmaster. Read on to learn how he approached the character.</p>\n<p><em>Warning! <strong>Major spoilers</strong> for the movie follow below.</em></p>\n<p><span id=\"more-682846\"></span></p>\n<p>In the last act of <em>Black Widow</em>, Natasha finds out Taskmaster is none other than Dreykov’s daughter, Antonia (<strong>Olga Kurylenko</strong>).  Natasha thought she killed Antonia years ago when she first tried to kill Dreykov. The connection between the two makes Taskmaster more than just a generic villain — she is not only an embodiment of the red on Natasha’s ledger, but a revelation that truly surprised the Avenger.</p>\n<p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-663577\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Black-Widow-Disney-700x300.jpg\" alt=\"Black Widow Disney+\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Black-Widow-Disney.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Black-Widow-Disney-360x154.jpg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<h2><strong>A Secret From Natasha’s Past</strong></h2>\n<p>&#8220;One of the great fears for Natasha Romanoff is, ‘Oh, I thought something that wasn&#8217;t right,’&#8221; Pearson told /Film in an <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/black-widow-eric-pearson-interview/\">interview</a>. &#8220;She knows everything. She&#8217;s in control. She’s very meticulous that way. It&#8217;s how she wants to live her life. The idea that there&#8217;s a secret is a problem. The idea that there&#8217;s a secret from her past is a bigger problem. The idea that there&#8217;s a secret from her past that is hidden and reaching out with a Terminator-like aggression.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Natasha and Taskmaster having this connection wasn’t Marvel’s initial plan for the character, Pearson explained.</p>\n<blockquote><p>&#8220;We always talked about Taskmaster being this straightforward physical villain. And then the idea of looping it into the greatest shame of [Natasha’s] past, that&#8217;s how I constructed it — I wanted Taskmaster to matter the most to [Natasha] as a character and fit our story that way.&#8221;</p></blockquote>\n<p>That connection causes Natasha to do everything she can to save Antonia/Taskmaster, even though Dreykov has programmed her to do nothing but try to kill her. Natasha succeeds in the end, and Antonia ends the film free from her father and in the care of the other Black Widows. Natasha was able to clear at least some of the red on her ledger, which makes for a bittersweet ending, knowing her sacrifice in <em>Endgame.</em></p>\n<p><em>Black Widow </em>is currently in theaters and available on Disney+ via Premier Access.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/black-widow-taskmaster-twist/\">How the &#8216;Black Widow&#8217; Screenwriter 02:35:16","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:16","title":"Groovy! ‘The Evil Dead’ Coming Back to Theaters for 40th Anniversary Event","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=682828","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-673397 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/evil-dead-rise-hbo-max-700x321.jpg\" alt=\"evil dead rise hbo max\" width=\"700\" height=\"321\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/evil-dead-rise-hbo-max-700x321.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/evil-dead-rise-hbo-max-360x165.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/evil-dead-rise-hbo-max-768x352.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/evil-dead-rise-hbo-max.jpg 948w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>Get out your denim and your chainsaws, because <em><strong>The Evil Dead </strong></em>is coming to theaters for one night only.</p>\n<p>Fathom Events and Grindhouse Releasing will bring writer/director <strong>Sam Raimi</strong>&#8216;s 1981 indie horror classic back to theaters nationwide for its 40th anniversary. The best part? The King himself, <strong>Bruce Campbell</strong>, Mr. Ashley Joanna &#8220;Ash&#8221; Williams, will give an exclusive introduction.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-682828\"></span></p>\n<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-621487 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Evil-Dead-9-700x301.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"700\" height=\"301\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Evil-Dead-9.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Evil-Dead-9-360x155.jpg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<h2>Bruce on the Big Screen</h2>\n<p><em>The Evil Dead</em> <em>40th Anniversary</em> comes to movie theaters nationwide on Thursday, <strong>October 7</strong>, at 7:30 p.m. (local time).</p>\n<p>In a recent interview with <a href=\"https://ew.com/movies/bruce-campbell-evil-dead-doctor-strange/\">Entertainment Weekly</a>, Campbell shared that while he&#8217;s excited about the anniversary celebration, he doesn&#8217;t exactly agree on the number of years it&#8217;s been.</p>\n<p>&#8220;I would not call it the 40th anniversary, I would call it the 42nd anniversary,&#8221; Campbell said. &#8220;There&#8217;s always a difference between when the movie actually got shot and when it came out. And with <em>Evil Dead</em>, it was about two or three years difference, because we filmed it in 1979, so we&#8217;re coming up on 42-odd years later. But it&#8217;s fun to celebrate it!&#8221;</p>\n<p>He also shared that his introduction is being shot at a drive-in theater, and that he&#8217;s thankful for the longevity of the film&#8217;s success.</p>\n<p>&#8220;I think it&#8217;s a good opportunity to look back and be grateful that it hung around this long. People still seem to want to watch it, they still seem to enjoy it. It&#8217;s on to the next generation now. It&#8217;s not even the original fans. Their children have been introduced to it now. So it&#8217;s my job just to keep perpetuating it.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Campbell also took a moment to warn fans of the sequels or television series that the first film is a completely different beast.</p>\n<p>&#8220;If you have not seen it, not much will prepare you for it! It is an unrated movie, so you have to be prepared for that. There are elements that some people would find very disturbing. So I would say it&#8217;s not for the faint of heart, but those people who like it, really like it,&#8221; he said. &#8220;Each movie of the three is weirdly different. The first <em>Evil Dead</em> is kind of a melodrama, a lot of laughs come out of the excessive effects and hokey dialogue, and bad acting.&#8221;</p>\n<p><img class=\"aligncenter wp-image-51935\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/zz6ea6e627.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"700\" height=\"1014\" /></p>\n<h2>The Groovy Details</h2>\n<p><em>The Evil Dead</em> spawned a whole franchise, including its sequels, <em><strong>Evil Dead </strong></em><em><strong>II </strong></em>and <em><strong>Army of Darkness</strong></em>, a 2013 remake/reboot, several video games, and the <strong><em>Ash vs. Evil Dead </em></strong>television series. The latest installment in the series, <em><strong>Evil Dead Rise</strong></em>, <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/evil-dead-rise-filming/\">began filming in June</a> and <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/evil-dead-rise-hbo-max/\">is set to come to HBOMax in the near future.</a></p>\n<p>Tickets for the 40th anniversary event <a href=\"https://www.fathomevents.com/events/The-Evil-Dead-40th-Anniversary\">go on sale <strong>Friday, August 13</strong> at www.FathomEvents.com</a> and through participating cinema box offices.</p>\n<p><em>The Evil Dead</em> follows Ash, his girlfriend Linda (<strong>Betsy Baker</strong>), his sister Cheryl (<strong>Ellen Sandweiss</strong>), and their friends Scotty (<strong>Hal Delrich</strong>) and Shelly (<strong>Sarah York</strong>). The young twenty-somethings drive out to a remote cabin in the woods for a fun vacation, only to discover the Book of the Dead inside the cabin. After reading some of the book out loud (inspiring a trope for years to come), they summon the forces of evil and must fight for their lives.</p>\n<p>In addition to the theatrical re-release, Lionsgate Home Entertainment will also be releasing a collectible box set of <em>The Evil Dead</em>, <em>Evil Dead II</em>, and all three seasons of <em>Ash vs. Evil Dead</em> this fall. More details to come.</p>\n<p>You can catch the Deadites in all of their gory glory on the big screen on <strong>October 7, 2021</strong><em>. </em></p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-evil-dead-coming-back-to-theaters-40th-anniversary/\">Groovy! &#8216;The Evil Dead&#8217; Coming Back to Theaters for 40th Anniversary Event</a> appeared first on 02:35:16","updatedTime":"2021-07-13 02:35:16","title":"‘The Witcher’ Season 2 Will Feature a More Talkative Geralt, Henry Cavill Teases","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=682806","description":"<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-643904 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-4-700x312.jpg\" alt=\"the witcher season 2 footage\" width=\"700\" height=\"312\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-4-700x312.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-4-360x161.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-4-768x342.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-4.jpg 1440w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>Get those coins ready to toss to your Witcher, because apparently he&#8217;s going to be a lot chattier this season.</p>\n<p>At the inagural WitcherCon on July 9, <strong>Henry Cavill</strong>, who plays the notoriously stoic witcher Geralt in Netflix&#8217;s <strong><em>The Witcher</em></strong>, sat down to talk about the series with reporter Josh Horowitz and answer some fan questions. He spoke at length about his character, and <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/the-witcher-season-2-trailer/\">all of the goodies we can expect in season 2</a>. But most importantly, he spoke about whether Geralt&#8217;s dialogue will expand beyond the occasional &#8220;hmph.&#8221;</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-682806\"></span></p>\n<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-651585 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-5-e1608670185110-700x318.jpg\" alt=\"the witcher bloopers\" width=\"700\" height=\"318\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-5-e1608670185110-700x318.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-5-e1608670185110-360x164.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-5-e1608670185110-768x349.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/the-witcher-5-e1608670185110.jpg 1218w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<h2>Not Just a Grumpy Snowman</h2>\n<p><em><strong>The Witcher</strong></em>&#8216;s Geralt is a man of few words in season one. He&#8217;s a stoic, gruff kind of hero that just doesn&#8217;t seem to have much important to say. In an interview with Horowitz for <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=joFSMgR-OEk&#38;feature=emb_title\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">WitcherCon</a>, Cavill said he thought it was important for him to speak less, since he was a fish out of water.</p>\n<p>&#8220;I played season 1 deliberately like that which was him in the wilds without the opportunity for vast wades of dialogue. I thought it best he speaks less because it looks like he thinks more. That was the intention then,&#8221; Cavill said.</p>\n<p>However, with Geralt returning to his home turf at Kaer Morhen, Cavill figured it was time for him to open up a bit.</p>\n<blockquote><p>&#8220;But in the season 2 scenario, he is in Kaer Morhen, his home space, amongst the people he knows, and, in my opinion, I was to be verbose and be intellectual because that is what he is. He’s not a brute. For me, it was very important to bring Geralt as close to the books as the vision and plot would allow. And I wanted him to be more verbose and more intellectual and more representative of a man who has lived seventy years and has a philosophical lean. He can be mopey at times, but he is also wise. He has been around. He is a nice guy despite having moments of unpleasantness, and capable of extraordinary violence, but his intentions are pure. I wanted to reflect on that as much as possible.&#8221;</p></blockquote>\n<p>Geralt is a man who has lived through quite a bit and has decades of experience to share, but sometimes he came across as a bit of an ice cube in the first season.</p>\n<p>&#8220;It is very easy to fall down the line of him being the grumpy snowman and there is a comedy aspect to that, and I wanted to lean away from it in Season 2,&#8221; Cavill elaborated.</p>\n<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-682287 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/witcher3-700x300.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/witcher3.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/witcher3-360x154.jpg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<h2>Some Complicated Relationships</h2>\n<p>Cavill also spoke at length about his character&#8217;s relationships, primarily with his foster daughter, Ciri, and his former flame, Yennefer. He felt it was important to make Geralt a good, caring figure while also highlighting that he&#8217;s a little out of his element with parenthood.</p>\n<p>&#8220;I wanted him to be warm. If not a father figure, then definitely an older brother figure and have this kind of caring nature to it. He is not going to be super soft because he has himself had a very hard life and she’s about to lead a very hard life. She is no longer a princess and so he is not going to be softening her up any further, but he is going straight down the line,&#8221; Cavill explained. &#8220;That was very important to me that Geralt has this sense of humor while also being tough and intellectual and wise and I tried to lean on it as much as possible when it came to the relationship with Cirilla.”</p>\n<p>While Cavill dodged questions about the nature of Geralt&#8217;s relationship with the sorceress Yennefer (<strong>Anya Chalotra</strong>), he did note how their split would effect Geralt in the coming season.</p>\n<p>&#8220;For me, it is important to draw from the source material and I wanted to keep it as close as possible to that. The loss of Yennefer is a great blow for Geralt, and it is something he buries deep, but he is a man of action, a wise man and his focus is Cirilla. He has lost plenty of people in his life, but he has a job to do and is focused more on being there for Cirilla.&#8221;</p>\n<p>You can watch the entire interview yourself below and hear it from the man himself. Season 2 of <em>The Witcher<strong> </strong></em>debuts on Netflix on <strong>December 17, 2021</strong>.</p>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\"
